1594202249|5.0|\\"It was like finding the gateway to the past unguarded, unforbidden because it didn't have to be.  Built into the act of return finally was this glittering mosaic of doubt.\\"<br /><br />Imagine a surfer-noir novel set in L.A. and environs in the spring of 1970 by Thomas Pynchon. Could anything be better? Answer: Inherent Vice is better! It's everything you could imagine, and then some. Every sentence is hugely entertaining, sort of the way Cormac McCarthy is entertaining, and also P.G. Wodehous. You just relish the diction. The plot is pure Chandler in its schematics: that is, characters are multiplied unnecessarily all for the dazzling color they give to the story; plot trots behind trying to keep up and just about succeeds in catching every character before they hit the ground. As in Chandler the characters who matter sort of only slowly emerge from the carnival, some very late: but that's the point of a carnival. Everyone matters to some gum-shoe or -sandal: we're concerned here with the ones that Marlowe or Doc are concerned with.<br /><br />And Pynchon is so different from the writers I've lately been thinking about, writers who narrate the experience of narration. Reading Pynchon is a little like reading Shakespeare or Hammett: I'm just a wide-eyed reader again, not a person figuring out the experience of writing this book.  I mean I am that sort of audience too for fun TV and Elmore Leonard and Neal Stephenson, for example. I like being just that. But for TV or Elmore Leonard I like because I can just enjoy the easy enjoyment of the thing. Whereas Pynchon requires -- no he doesn't require, he REWARDS -- the same kind of concentration I give Woolf or Geoff Dyer: a lot of concentration.  But there's something wonderful about not concentrating on the experience of the book as an act of writing.  You just concentrate for your own pleasure.  And, man, he writes a good last line.<br /><br />It's not that Pynchon as writer isn't an issue in the book.  He is.  But he's astonishingly good natured as a writer.  What other real writers are?  I can't think of any, not even Fielding.<br /><br />Anyhow the first half of the book (when it's explaining something) repeats  very occasionally -- sparsely even -- and very hauntingly two versions of the same phrase: \\"in those days\\"; \\"at that time.\\"  This plus a reference to Vineland as a place, plus one bracketed movie date at the end, are the only authorial self-references, the only sense that you have that an author is telling this to the reader instead of the voice coming off the page as part of the laid-back generosity of the narrative atmosphere.  I love those phrases.  They disappear from the second half, which is part of the very sadness the book's about: the end of an era, with Manson & Nixon (Doc must be called Doc partly as a descendent of Mason's, one of whose sons is called Doctor Isaac.)<br /><br />Doc is slightly younger than Pynchon himself, pushing thirty in 1970, as we learn in a great, casually sad passage:<br /><br />\\"Plastic trikes in the yards, people out watering the flowers and working on their cars, kids in the driveways shooting hoops, the high-frequency squeal of a TV sweep circuit through a screen door as Doc came up the path of the address he was looking for, to be followed by the more worldly sound, as he reached the front steps, of The Bugs Bunny/Road Runner Hour.  According to Fritz, the sweep frequency was 15,750 cycles per second, and the instant Doc turned thirty, which would be any minute now, he would no longer be able to hear it.  So this routine of American house approach had begun to hold for him a particular sadness.\\"  (P. 199)<br /><br />Yes, I remember that routine.  I thought TV's didn't do that anymore.  But they do. Pynchon remembers this sound, which he can no longer hear, from forty-two years ago, when he reached the untrustworthy age of 30.<br /><br />And yet the novel is entirely without self-pity, which might be why some of the smartest people I know don't like Doc much as a character: he's not made for pity: the book doesn't pity him either, it just lovingly recreates \\"those days,\\" \\"that time\\" (\\"That time, O times!\\" as Cleopatra says) -- a time of endless betrayal as all times are for Pynchon, but a time when there were so many people to betray.  The stoned hippie belief in innocence is ridiculous and yet something to cherish.  Doc is cynical as all get-out, but still cherishes it, which means he's part of the innocence he's cynical about.  He gets all this and still likes his world.  You don't think people can really be like that? He gets all this and still likes his world.  You don't think people can really be like that?  Pynchon is.
0307269981|5.0|OK, if you read Stieg Larsson's \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" jolt-fest, there is no way to skip this year's blistering \\"The Girl Who Played with Fire.\\"  But then you've got to wade through about 140 pages of \\"Dragon Tattoo\\" recap and kinky sex history of the cast - yeah, Stieg, we get it - Sweden is sexually liberated - before hunkering down to the really good stuff.  But trust me, this is a small price to pay as the prelude to the remaining 340 pages of over-the-top thrills and suspense, a rockin' and rolling mystery that would make the Vikings proud, a smorgasbord a violence and head games wrapped around the most interesting protagonist to hit the pages since, well, \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo.\\"<br /><br />That would be Lisbet Salander, the damaged genius created by the late Stieg Larsson for stopped too soon at a trilogy - the pierced and tattooed misfit with an extreme distaste for misogynists, a healthy disrespect for authority honed by years of institutional abuse, and a deliciously bad attitude.  In this installment, the author metes out in frustratingly small doses the brutal baggage that created the flawed brilliance of Salander, a cleverly - if improbably rendered- female combination of Kevin Mitnick, Bruce Lee, and Ozzie Osbourne.  Following the Blomkvist-Salander team that broke the decades-old Vanger mystery of \\"Tattoo\\" and Salander's subsequent heist of  corrupt industrialist Hans-Erik Wennerstrom which has left her financially independent, Blomkvist and his \\"Millennium\\" magazine take on a freelance journalist and his expose of a Eastern Europe-to-Sweden prostitution ring that catches a lot of pols and cops in high places with their pants around their ankles.  But when the researchers end up with bullets in their heads, fired from a gun with Salander's prints all over it, a frenzied media and biased prosecution team have all of Sweden looking for a slandered Salanader as the obvious perp.  But an eventually skeptical cop and the faithful Blomkvist stand by their maverick, setting up an over-the-top, sometimes poignant, mostly unbelievable, but always entertaining shock-lit romp through Europe's frozen playground of the north, never pausing long enough to note the irony in the tyranny of Sweden's social utopia theory against an all too real backdrop of heavy-handed state control which put poor Salander in her predicament in the first place.  Larsson zigs and zags through a number of unexpected twists and turns, through lots of stops in 7-11 (is Swedish cuisine so bad that 7-11 is really a preferred dining spot?) leading to the most white-knuckled climax since Josh Bazell's Dr. Peter Brown pulls a fibula in the frenzied \\"Beat the Reaper\\".<br /><br />Paradoxically, Larsson does not read like stoic fatalism of Scandinavian kin Irdridason, Fosset, or Nesbo, but rather more like the raucous, unapologetic, and graphically violent prose of Charlie Huston, Duane Swierczynski, or Adrian McKinty - high praise from this reviewer.  Yet Larsson has some axes to grind - violence against women, corruption, social injustice - but does so tastefully without beating the reader into submission with misguided indignities.  He wraps his thriller around a clever mathematical theme, adding to the depth and intelligence of a well-crafted novel that entertains on the surface with raw adrenaline, while satisfying less visceral emotions at a deeper level.  It is a shame that Stieg Larsson is no longer around to continue this refreshing and creative series, but I'll anxiously await the English release of  the final chapter in \\"The Girl Who Kicked the Hornet's Nest.\\"and by their maverick, setting up an over-the-top, sometimes poignant, mostly unbelievable, but always entertaining shock-lit romp through Europe's frozen playground of the north, never pausing long enough to note the irony in the tyranny of Sweden's social utopia theory against an all too real backdrop of heavy-handed state control which put poor Salander in her predicament in the first place. 0307277453|5.0|This book by Neil Shubin does a wonderful job of portraying how the diverse organisms on earth, both past and present, are profoundly related in their forms and functions because of a shared family tree stretching back to the origins of life, more than 3 billion years ago.  A central focus of the book is how humans are anatomically related to other organisms, but a wide variety of other interesting relationships are presented as well.<br /><br />Shubin crafts this rich portrait by expertly weaving together a wide array of details from many areas of biology including paleontology, comparative anatomy, evolutionary biology, developmental biology, molecular biology, and genetics.  In this regard, although I've studied plenty of biomedicine over the years, including graduate coursework in mammalian physiology, I was pleasantly surprised by how much fascinating new material I learned.<br /><br />At the same time, the book is very well written, with clear explanations and a narrative which often unfolds like a detective novel, so the book should be approachable by any general reader comfortable with science.  And speaking of detective novels, the book also does a great job of giving an insider's feel for how science is actually done, including the importance of persistence and luck.  I was particularly amazed by how often diverse lines of evidence turned out to tightly converge, thereby enabling Shubin to make a strong case for the various (and sometimes counterintuitive) ideas he presents.<br /><br />If I had to critique the book, I would prefer that it included more figures, used a somewhat more textbook-like format (to make it easier to go back and find information), and included more scientific terminology (to connect the book more with the terminology I'm already familiar with).  But, again, these are preferences, and the book is still excellent as is.<br /><br />Overall, I highly recommend this book to anyone interested in natural history, particularly the evolutionary history of the anatomical developments leading to humans.  I also particularly recommend this book to people who have already taken a standard course in human anatomy, including health care professionals, since Shubin provides a different perspective which nicely complements such a course.  While a standard course describes the \\"what\\" of human anatomy, this book explains the fascinating \\"why,\\" including why we're anatomically predisposed to experience certain diseases (obesity, heart disease, diabetes, varicose veins, hemorrhoids, choking, sleep apnea, hiccups, inguinal hernias, knee problems, etc.).<br /><br />Also, readers may want to consider [[ASIN:0674418050 Human Structure]] by Cartmill et al for a much more detailed treatment of human gross anatomy (but no histology or molecular cell biology) in an evolutionary, functional, comparative, and developmental framework.velopments leading to humans.  I also particularly recommend this book to people who have already taken a standard course in human anatomy, including health care professionals, since Shubin provides a different perspective which nicely complements such a course.  While a standard course describes the \\"what\\" of human anatomy, this book explains the fascinating \\"why,\\" including why we're anatomically predisposed to experience certain diseases (obesity, heart disease, diabetes, varicose veins, hemorrhoids, choking, sleep apnea, hiccups, inguinal hernias, knee problems, etc.).    Also, readers may want to consider [[ASIN:0674418050 Human Structure]] by Cartmill et al for a much more detailed treatment of human gross anatomy (but no histology or molecular cell biology) in an evolutionary, functional, comparative, and developmental framework.

B0025KVLTM|4.0|I love the Beatles. I love good audio. The thought of new and improved Beatles got me to purchase 3 remastered CDs (Rubber Soul, Sgt. Pepper and Abbey Road) on the first day of release. After listening with my trusty Sennheiser HD580 headphones, what's the verdict?<br /><br />Listening to the re-mastered CDs is like looking out a window that has just been cleaned. The view is the same, but it is sharper, with more detail. The SOUND is the same, but there is more attack and decay (the 'shape' of the beginning and end of sounds), especially on vocals and acoustic instruments.<br /><br />The sound stage is more spacious. McCartney's bass work on 'Day in the Life' is more melodious. 'Lovely Rita' has even more little hidden sounds at the end. When Lennon says 'bye' at the beginning of the Sargent Pepper Reprise it is clearer.<br /><br />So what's not to like? Nothing, really. It does not feel overproduced, compressed or 'sweetened'. It feels like the record the Beatles would have made with better audio equipment. It's not going to bowl you over. It's the same music. It just sounds better.<br /><br />Some people have said that they cannot tell the difference between the two versions. I have a pretty good audio system, and the difference was noticeable, but not dramatic. If you have a lower end audio system the differences may not be worth the purchase price if you own the original CDs.<br /><br />But if you don't own the CDs, for goodness sakes, the remastered version is better. And if you have a pretty good stereo, the difference is worth the price of admission.

0439023491|5.0|I confess, I ignored The Hunger Games for months.  I figured it was another author's feeble attempt to pull off some zombie/vampire/werewolf novel a la Stephanie Meyers.  In the months following the release of Breaking Dawn, I'd become weary of searching the bookstore's shelves for something decent to read.<br /><br />But after reading the reviews on here -- and noting, with some surprise, the lack of one-starred reviews -- I took the leap and bought The Hunger Games.  Figured I'd return it in a day or so after reading it.<br /><br />I was bewildered.  Then excited.  Then...<br /><br />There are no words that could fully describe how good these books are.  I've read both books in two days.  Suzanne Collins is brilliantly amazing!  Her books will forcibly pull you into the dark nation of Panem.  You will not just read, you will EXPERIENCE what happens in each chapter.  You will become a fellow citizen of Katniss.<br /><br />To avoid giving away any spoilers, I'm just going to review the experience of reading.<br /><br />I guess the main concerns for readers would be A) these are violent books, B) there is no fluffy romance, and C) some of what you read could be classified as horror.  Allow me to address these:<br /><br />A) Yes, these are violent books.  But the main character is not out to hurt people for the sake of killing.  She really isn't.  She's technically a pawn in the hands of a dictator, yet every action she makes is calculated to either save her own life or the life of someone she cares for.  These books are about weighing the costs between survival and sacrificial love -- for example, Katniss throws caution to the wind in The Hunger Games when one of her friends, a little girl, is screaming for help.  Rather than remain hidden, Katniss bursts out into the open and sprints to her friend's side.  The scene that follows is grippingly powerful and reaps longlasting effects on the people of Panem.<br /><br />B) Yes, there is no fluff.  That's because Suzanne Collins has effectively created a heroine who actually deals with realistic feelings.  There is literally no time for Katniss to sigh blissfully over the butterflies in her heart, or how drop-dead gorgeous Gale is, or whether Peeta's biceps are larger than Gale's.  She does not go into endless, lustful daydreams.  If anything, she shows embarrassment at nudity, which is refreshing.<br />There are, admittedly, moments where she recognizes the thrill of love.  Katniss will confess when she finds someone physically attractive.  The nice thing about her is that she balances the physical with the personality.<br /><br />So, yeah.  No sugary sweetness.  No fluff.  But true love?  Yes.<br /><br />C)  The horror factor:<br />Yes, these books do have an element of horror.  But what I find encouraging is that the horror is not without hope.  Katniss has something to fight against and she has allies.  That, to me, makes all the difference between a random bloodbath like Final Destination and something a little more poignant like Gladiator.<br /><br />Altogether, these books are the best I've read in a looong time!  I think the way Suzanne Collins ended Catching Fire was remarkably bold -- and I'm wondering now how I will ever last till she brings out the next book!  Five million kudos to Suzanne. B001PPLJIQ|4.0|The film was nominated for Best Picture, Best Director and Best Adapted Screenplay as well as a nomination for Kate Winslet for Best Actress and is based on Bernhard Schlink's novel of the same name.      It is the story of a young fifteen year old boy on the verge of sexual maturity, Michael Berg, living in the days of adjustment in Germany in the few years following World War II. He comes from an upper-class home with educated parents. While coming home from school, he becomes sick and vomits on the doorstep of a woman who helps him get home. This woman, Hanna, is a central character in the film. He has scarlet fever and after 3 months recovers from the illness, but something about the woman has captured his attention, even when he was ill, and he returns to thank her for her kindness. This somewhat unlikely pair begins an affair. Michael has a sexual awakening but also full romantic attachment to Hanna. She on the other hand seems very isolated and her motivations are more dark and uncertain.  When Michael complains about school, she invites him to read to her and soon they are making love and reading on a regular basis. The two go on a picnic where Hanna asks Michael to read the menu for her. Both Hanna and Michael are physically beautiful and the attraction seems natural even though she is twice his age. But then Hanna disappears and moves out of town and Michael is totally heartbroken as only a young first love can be hurt. We see him interacting with his peers, becoming sexually active, but always a bit more aloof and removed and unreachable than his peers. Many years later, while attending law school, he enrolls in a seminar that requires that he attend some trials of war criminals and sees that Hanna is one of those being prosecuted. It turns out that Hanna was a prison guard at a woman's prison where a terrible fire broke out and the guards would not release the women, condemning them to death.  At this point, it appears that maybe Hanna was seeking something innocent and fresh in her love affair with Michael, a sensation that could not last as she tries to escape her past and her guilt. Thus for her the affair was about lust for a beautiful bright and innocent young man as a way to regain her own lost innocence.      As the trial proceeds Michael is ambivalent in his feelings toward Hanna, having a difficult time separating the anger he feels for her leaving him with the anger he may feel for her war crime.  He is not caught between his own feelings of right and wrong, for he knows what she did was wrong. He is caught between his personal anger toward her for running away and his more intellectual concerns around the crimes and her role in them.    Michael will need to forgive her to emotionally move onward with his life. During the trial, Michael learns something that could make a real difference in the length of Hanna's sentence. Yet he does not act and there is considerable ambivalence here for we are uncertain if he fails to act because he wishes to protect Hanna's dignity or because he has yet to forgive her and thus is removed from her fate. There may also be a bit of revenge acting here as Michael has information that could impact the sentence given to Hanna and yet he withholds the information.     But if we are unable to forgive, who do we really hurt?  Michael was once a victim of an older woman who had sex with him as a boy and then left him when he was most in love. But the inability to forgive Hanna turns Michael into his own tormentor.      Director Stephen Daldry does a perfect job at capturing the cool world of post WW II Germany. Kate Winslet and David Kross are excellent. Ralph Fiennes is the only disappoint for he plays David too coldly, too icy, too removed and emotionless. Once again David Hare has done an exceptional adaptation of the novel into the screenplay.     The film is thoughtful, well acted, beautifully photographed, and remains a rich puzzle even after you have seen it to the end.
